Question No 24 by Ioannis Theonas (H−0367/97)

Subject: Failure of the Council to take specific measures in favour of SMUs

The proposal for a programme aimed at guaranteeing loans granted to small and medium‐sized undertakings in respect of investments which create jobs (Elise) is continuing to be blocked in the Council. The relevant appropriations are also being blocked in the Community budget, owing to the absence of a legal basis. Furthermore, the failure to take specific measures to create a favourable climate for the smallest SMUs is making thousands of SMUs bankrupt and leading to the loss of even more jobs.

Will the Council say why the adoption of the Elise programme is being delayed, and whether it agrees that the situation facing SMUs today, and in particular the smallest SMUs, makes it urgently necessary to adopt specific initiatives at Community level to address the consequences of the EU's competition policy as well as measures to facilitate the access of SMUs to sources of external funding on accessible terms, within the framework of a more general policy aimed at supporting productive investments, averting the loss of yet more jobs and reinforcing the productive base in peripheral and remote regions?

Answer

The Council examined at its meeting on 11 November 1996 the Commission proposal to guarantee loans for investment by SMEs (Elise). During this discussion it became apparent that under the present circumstances the required unanimous support for the scheme did not exist.

As the Honourable Member of Parliament is aware, the European Council of Dublin concluded that the fight against unemployment remains the prime responsibility of the Member States. Additionally, the European Council considered this issue as a top priority for the Union. Building on the Essen strategy to combat unemployment, the European Council adopted the Dublin Declaration on Employment that stresses the need for a continuation of the macroeconomic strategy for economic growth and employment, increased efforts to modernize the markets and exploit new sources of employment, a focusing on labour market efficiency and on investment in human resources, making taxation and social protection systems more employment‐friendly and strengthening the interplay between macroeconomic and structural policies in the Member States' Multiannual Employment Programmes.

Most actions in the context of the above guidelines are particularly beneficial to the SMEs. The same European Council stressed once again the essential role of the Internal Market in promoting growth and employment in Europe and the crucial role to this effect from the enhancement of the competitiveness of SMEs. SMEs will also greatly benefit from the extension of the Simpler Legislation for the Internal Market (SLIM) initiative.

The implementation of this policy should make a substantial contribution towards creating the economic conditions for the development of European SMEs. Furthermore, recognizing the particular importance of SMEs for the European economy, the Union has already provided a framework for financial support through the EIB, the EIF and the structural funds.

Subject: IGC review of Euratom Statutes and powers of oversight by the European Parliament

What considerations have been given in the Dutch Presidency's draft on the proposals of the Inter‐Governmental Conference for a revision of the European Atomic Energy Agency (EURATOM) statutes and the inclusion of extended powers of oversight by the European Parliament over EURATOM's activities and priorities?

Answer

In answer to Mr Smith's question on the review of the Treaty establishing the European Atomic Energy Community, I must once again stress two aspects.

In view of the above observations I am accordingly unable to answer the question put by the Honourable Member.

Question No 26 by Inger Schörling (H−0372/97)

Subject: Transport of live animals for slaughter

In recent weeks, people in Sweden have been demonstrating on the streets to express their disgust at the way in which live animals for slaughter are transported.

The directives on the protection during transport of live animals for slaughter is not being complied with. They are insufficient to ensure that the animals do not suffer during transport. Pursuant to Directive 95/29/EC(1) , the duration of transport may not exceed eight hours, after which a 24‐hour rest must be provided. Exceptions to this rule are possible if the vehicles used comply with very specific conditions. Such conditions were to be laid down in a separate directive. As this has not yet happened, anyone can take advantage of the exception. The limits laid own in Directive 95/29/EC are therefore meaningless.

It is a well known fact that animals for slaughter do not fare well during transport, that they may become stressed, be injured and become sick, all of which also has an effect on the quality of their meat. Does the Council consider there to be any justification at all for transporting live animals for slaughter over long distances?

Does it intend to promote a system under which animals are slaughtered as close as possible to the place in which they were reared? If so, how?

Will the Council take steps to end the granting of EU economic aid in respect o the transport of live animals for slaughter.

Answer

1. In reply to Mrs Schörling's question I must stress that the Council adopted on 29 June 1995 Directive 95/29/EEC with the aim of complementing the existing provisions on the protection of animals during transportation as laid down in Directive 91/628/EEC.

That Directive, which was due to be implemented by the Member States before 31 December 1996, lays down provisions which the Council regarded as necessary at that stage to see that animals were protected and to promote the welfare of all animals during transportation, irrespective of their species or category, so that each animal should have the same conditions as regards welfare. The directive provides:

(a) in the Annex to Chapter VII that the basic eight‐hour journeys for the transportation of animals may be exceeded if the vehicles used comply with specific conditions;

(b) in Article 5 that the competent authorities in the various Member States shall be obliged to grant the transporters a valid authorization for the transportation of animals subject to compliance with the requirements laid down in the legislation.

It is therefore in the first instance the responsibility of the Member States to see that these requirements are respected and to impose penalties in the event of any breach in accordance with the provisions of Article 18 of the Directive.

2. Moreover, in order to complement the existing provisions, Article 13 of the Directive provides:

(a) in the first paragraph that the Council should, on the basis of a proposal from the Commission, set further standards to be met by the means of transport. A Commission proposal to that effect is expected in the next few weeks;

(b) in the second paragraph that the Council should, on a proposal from the Commission, establish Community criteria which the stopping places must meet. The Council is currently well advanced in its consideration of a Commission proposal. The Presidency expects to be able to put everything in train to obtain a decision on the matter before the end of the first half of 1997.

3. Moreover, a proposal for a regulation has just been submitted to the Council aimed at amending Regulation (EEC) No 805/68 on the common organization of the market in the beef and veal sector in order to establish a link between the granting of export refunds and respect for the provisions on welfare. The Council is due to decide upon this proposal in the very near future.

4. The Council has not so far received any other Commission proposals on the subject of welfare.

Question No 27 by Hervé Fabre‐Aubrespy (H−0374/97)

Subject: European Community expenditure on information, communication and education

As budgetary authority responsible for authorizing information measures funded by the Community budget, how can the Council ensure that information campaigns conducted by the European Communities, or with their resources, do not interfere with national laws, especially in the context of national election campaigns? How can it ensure that such measures do not consist in unilaterally disseminating a particular view on a controversial issue, that they are conducted in close association with all partners and that they respect the principle of financial transparency?

Answer

In reply to this question by Mr Fabre‐Aubespy on European Community expenditure on information, communication and education, I must confirm that the European Parliament and the Council are the institutions which draw up the Community budget. However, pursuant to Article 205 of the EEC Treaty the Commission is empowered to implement on its own responsibility and within the limits of the appropriations without Council interference.

Moreover, as regards the Commission's activities in the field of information or other fields, the Treaty provides for various means to be made available to the European Parliament to exercise political control over that institution.

Question No 28 by Anne McIntosh (H−0202/97)

Subject: European Union measures to combat paedophilia

Will the Council provide an update on the progress of the multi‐annual programme (1996‐2000) to combat the problem of paedophilia? Is action taking place to facilitate the exchange of information between Member States on known offenders?

Specifically, what action has been taken to ensure that the propagation of material related to the sexual exploitation of children is not accessible on the Internet?

In what ways can the Council envisage increased cooperation between Member States, either judicial or practical, to help prevent such material appearing on the Internet?

Answer

On 29 November 1996, the Council adopted a Joint Action establishing an incentive and exchange programme for persons responsible for combating trade in human beings and sexual exploitation of children. It is the Commission which is responsible for the implementation of this programme and it shall report annually to the European Parliament and the Council on its implementation.

On 16 December 1996, the Council adopted a Joint Action extending the mandate given to the Europol Drugs Unit. When the Joint Action entered into force, the facilities of the EDU became available to the Member States. This includes exchange of information, support of operations and analytical support. The EDU has already dealt with several requests from Member States in this field.

As to material accessible on the Internet, this question is currently studied by the Council, with a view to selfregulation within the Member States and for purposes of enhancing judicial cooperation in this area. The Member States so far have little or no experience of offences perpetrated through the Internet but it is clear that material which is illegal outside the Internet should also be illegal when the material is on the Internet.

Therefore, the problem of the Internet cannot be seen in isolation. The most difficult problem is the actual control of how this type of material is disseminated and the possible conflicts that may arise with general principles of freedom of information.

Question No 29 by Jan Andersson (H−0249/97)

Subject: Nickel‐free euro coins

Nickel causes allergic reactions in many people in Europe. It is to be found inter alia in coins. Now that the Union is to have a common currency, it is important that the new coins do not cause allergic reactions.

How does the Netherlands Presidency intend to ensure that the new euro coins are nickel‐free?

Answer

In answer to Mr Andersson's very interesting question, I would like to point out that the Council has examined the issue of nickel sensitivity very carefully. As a result, the Council has adopted a Directive (94/27/EC) that limits the use of nickel in products that are in direct and prolonged contact with the skin.

The mint directors have presented a proposal where at least four of the eight coins are nickel free. Alternative nickel free materials are being considered also for the remaining coins. However, there might be technical problems with the bi‐coloured one euro and two euro coins and in these cases further testing will be required.

The Council has not yet decided upon the issue which will be resolved in the framework of a formal proposal by the Commission based on Article 105a(2) of the Treaty.

At the ECOFIN Council last Monday, the Council was informed by Commissioner de Silguy of the state of play concerning the preparation of the Euro coins in particular with regard to the outstanding questions relating to their technical characteristics and design: the nickel content of certain coins (10 and 20 euro cents) and the shape of 20 euro cents coins.

Commissioner de Silguy indicated that, after finalizing consultations with associations of consumers and of the blind as well as with vending machines producers, his Institution – taking into account the outcome of these consultations and based on the results of the design competition – will present to the next ECOFIN Council in June a formal proposal for the Euro coins.

II. Questions to the Commission

 

Question No 39 by Glenys Kinnock (H−0323/97)

Subject: EU relations with Nicaragua

Is the Commission aware that President Arnaldo Aleman and the Liberal Alliance in Nicaragua have introduced a bill which is intended to dissolve the Institute of Women and which will create a Ministry of the Family? Is the Commission also aware that, if this bill becomes law, it will represent a serious attack on the rights of women and children in Nicaragua?

In view of the Commission's and Council's stated position on gender rights, will effective and immediate action be taken to reflect the concern which is justifiably felt about this proposed legislation in Nicaragua?
Answer

The European Commission cannot enter into a debate about internal administrative and political decisions taken by a sovereign government.

Nevertheless, the Commission is concerned about the future of INIM as an autonomous national women's institute, which up till now has had its own legal personality and financial independence.

The Commission does not have sufficient elements to judge at this time whether the draft law would undermine the national instruments for the advancement of women in Nicaragua. INIM is a counterpart institution for ECfunded projects (and those of several member states) including a major programme of support to adolescent women and young single mothers (4 Mecu). The Commission will closely monitor any repercussions arising from qualitative changes in the approach of the Nicaraguan government to the issue of women's empowerment which might adversely affect any projects or initiatives and it retains the right to withdraw its support.

The Commission has been informed that representatives of the UNPD, the Netherlands and Sweden are to have a meeting with the Nicaraguan Minister of Foreign Affairs. During this meeting the representatives will transmit their concerns about the possible loss of momentum of the INIM as an independent institution that has, until now, promoted women's rights in Nicaragua and they will also underline their attachment to the Beijing principles in the framework of their cooperation. The Commission fully supports this political initiative.

Question No 40 by Bernd Posselt (H−0353/97)

Subject: Negotiations with Egypt

How have relations between the EU and Egypt, in particular the negotiations on the association agreement, been developing in the light of the Mediterranean conference and how does the Commission assess the political situation in this important partner country?

Answer

A new Association Agreement requires major economic legislative and administrative adjustments in the partner countries. In the case of Egypt the scale of economic reforms to be undertaken and the issue of agriculture, which presents problems different from those encountered with the Maghreb countries and with Israel, also explain the duration of the negotiations.

As concerns the specific chapter of agriculture, Egypt in the last months has revised to a great extent its original requests for concessions in agricultural trade. However, because of the high level of the original demands, the revised figures remain still distant from what the Community could grant, taking into account the present market difficulties for the main products concerned (potatoes, rice, oranges, cut flowers).

Differences also exist in other chapters such as re‐admission of illegal immigrants and social benefits for workers. However, they are not unbridgeable gaps.

Both sides agree on the strategic, long‐term importance of the agreement and are committed to reach conclusions as soon as possible.

The Commission will spare no effort to achieve a balanced agreement in the interest of both parties, hopefully within the first semester of this year.

Question No 41 by Marie‐Paule Kestelijn‐Sierens (H−0362/97)

Subject: Demarcation of indigenous area Raposa/Serra do Sol in Brazil

In order to meet the opposition (decree no. 1775) of non‐Indian people occupying land and the government of Roraima to the FUNAI's original proposals for the demarcation of the indigenous area of Raposa/Terra do Sol, in late 1996 the Brazilian government submitted a new proposal for demarcation which would reduce the indigenous area by one fifth (or 300 000 hectares).

Is the Commission aware of this controversial proposal on the part of the Brazilian government? What action will the Commission take to make known its displeasure at this proposal? Is it not possible for the Commission to demand withdrawal of this unacceptable proposal as a condition for financial aid for the demarcation of indigenous areas in the framework of the model projects in Brazil?

Answer

The problem of the demarcation of Brazilian indigenous areas is a slow and complex process which began in the early years of the twentieth century and which is not yet finished.

According to the information available to the Commission the Brazilian indigenous areas are home to approximately 250 000 Indians who occupy about 11.18 % of the national territory covering an area of over 950 000 km² , that is more than the United Kingdom and France put together.

In December 1996 the Minister for Justice decided that the demarcation of the Raposa Serra do Sol area should amount to 1.4 million hectares rather than the 1.68 million hectares initially proposed. That solution makes it possible to decompartmentalize the non‐Indian towns (in particular Normandia) which were surrounded in the initial project and to respect the rights of the indigenous population for whom a per capita area of over one thousand hectares is reserved.

The Commission is following this delicate question closely. It fervently hopes that the indigenous people will have the benefit, as we are beginning to see, of dignified living conditions on properly demarcated territory. It supports the efforts launched but cannot interfere directly in the demarcation operations to discuss the rights and wrongs of various measures.

Moreover, the Commission is assisting the indigenous peoples by participating in various projects and programmes and by ensuring that favourable conditions are created for utilizing the land with a view to sustainable and durable development.

***

Question No 49 by Jan Andersson (H−0318/97)

Subject: Competition in the European shipbuilding industry

Europe's shipbuilding industry has to compete for markets against international competition. In a number of Member States, the shipbuilding industry receives government aid, whilst in other it does not, and this situation distorts competition.

What steps will the Commission take to prevent aid to shipbuilding from distorting competition?

Answer

State aid to shipbuilding is subject to special rules (currently the 7th Directive) which ensure equity and transparency throughout the Community. The validity of the Directive ends on 31 December 1997.

An international agreement, commonly known as the OECD agreement, has been signed by the Community, the USA, Japan, Korea and Norway, respecting normal competitive conditions in the commercial shipbuilding and ship repair industry. All parties to the Agreement have ratified it with the exception of the USA. Without their ratification the Agreement cannot enter into force.

For the future, the preference of the Community would be the entry into force of the Agreement. However, failing this and while the Community continues to await the eventual ratification of the Agreement by the USA, in its meeting on 24 April 1997, the Council has acknowledged the need for a ‚specific new regime’ for the shipbuilding sector together with the extension of the Seventh Directive until 31 December 1998 and requested the Commission to present a proposal in this sense by this summer.

An amendment to the current rules which will provide a legal basis for aid to be allowed for the restructuring of yards in Germany, Greece and Spain has also received the political approval from the Council on 24 April.

These restructuring programmes are the same as had already been initiated and approved earlier by the Commission and the Council but unforeseeable circumstances made it necessary to increase the amounts of public funding. However, extra reductions in capacity are offered and a tight, monitoring procedure will be put into place. There is also by the respective governments a commitment that this is the last time these yards will receive restructuring aid.

***

Question No 50 by Hubert Pirker (H−0363/97)

Subject: Application of EC competition law to SMEs

In many regions of Europe small and medium‐sized enterprises are of crucial importance for economic development and employment. In order to make them more competitive with large enterprises and in view of the negligible effect of their behaviour on the Common Market, they should be largely exempt from EC competition law.

What action is the Commission intending to take to extend the existing exemptions for small and medium‐sized enterprises?

Answer

For small and medium‐sized enterprises a competitive economic environment is very important. It is clearly in their interest for competition not to be restricted or distorted by private action such as the creation of market dominance through mergers or by governmental measures such as state aid to big companies. In this respect, the Commission as the European competition authority is a ‚natural ally’ of small and medium‐sized enterprises. In maintaining effective competition the Commission indirectly protects them.

Community competition rules rarely affect small and medium‐sized enterprises directly. For instance, they normally fall short of Community merger control due to the relatively high turnover thresholds. This will not change after the amendment as principally agreed upon in the Council on 34 April. With respect to Articles 85 and 86 the Commission has published a draft de minimis notice. De minimis defines the thresholds up to which the Commission does not assume an appreciable restriction of competition. This is of particular interest for small and medium‐sized enterprises. At the moment, in order to benefit, parties must have both a combined market share of less than 5 % and a turnover of less than ECU 300 million. The new draft applies a higher market share for vertical agreements (10 %) and has dropped the turnover threshold altogether. The draft includes a specific reference to small and medium‐sized enterprises(2) . It says that cooperation between them would rarely appreciably affect trade between Member States.

With regard to State aid, the Commission's general policy towards aid to promote SMEs has always been positive. Although State aid for SMEs is entirely subject to the State aid rules of Article 92‐93, the Commission's appreciation of the compatibility of aid with the Common Market will take into account the characteristics of SMEs and the specific problems they face. The Commission therefore published guidelines on State aid for SMEs (OJ 1996 C 213) spelling out the concrete criteria that aid to SMEs must fulfil in order to be allowed under the exemption of Article 92(3)c. Generally speaking, it is required that the aid has an incentive character, is proportionate to the handicaps SMEs are confronted with and is necessary to achieve objectives in the Community interest which market forces alone would not secure. The guidelines translate these conditions into concrete admissible aid purposes and aid intensities.

Besides, several frameworks and guidelines regarding other types of aid (e.g. research and development aid, employment aid, environmental aid) contain more favourable rules for the assessment of compatibility if the aid is given to SMEs.

Finally, the Commission's de minimis rule for State aid (OJ 1996 C 68) is of particular interest to SMEs. According to this rule, aid not exceeding ECU 100 000 over a three‐year period is deemed not to have a perceptible impact on trade and competition between Member States and is therefore not subject to prior notification to the Commission.

Question No 51 by Astrid Thors (H−0364/97)

Subject: Deregulation of the telecommunications sector

The Commission has drawn up a number of provisions concerning the application of the general rules of competition in the telecommunications sector, including timetables for the measures which should be taken in order that free competition may be implemented, as already decided, on 1 January 1998.

A number of Member States have reportedly requested extensions to the deadlines for some of the measures that are required to deregulate the sector, and implementation of the relevant legislation is proceeding slowly in those countries. What steps will the Commission take to ensure that Member States comply with the rules and keep to the agreed timetable?

Answer

According to the Commission Directive 96/19/EC(3) with regard to the implementation of full competition in the telecommunications markets, Member States with less developed networks shall be granted upon request an additional implementation period of up to five years and Member States with very small networks, an additional implementation period of up to two years, for the liberalization of voice telephony and for the establishment and provision of public telcommunications networks, provided it is needed to achieve the necessary structural adjustments.

Ireland, Portugal, Luxembourg, Spain and Greece have requested such additional implementation periods to Ireland (27 November 1996) and Portugal (12 November 1996). The Commission will adopt formal decisions on the other requests during Spring 1997.

The Commission will monitor that the timetable is respected by these Member States. In cases of nonimplementation the Commission will initiate infringement proceedings as provided by Article 169 of the EC Treaty. The Commission will also monitor closely the implementation of all other aspects of the liberalization and harmonization package adopted by the Community in the field of telecommunications and initiate proceedings in the case of failure to implement.

Since the Commission Directive as implemented by the relevant Decisions have direct effect, individuals have the right to bring action against Member States in national courts for breach of Community law if any of these Member States would fail to correctly transpose the Directive. Moreover, according to the Case law of the Court of Justice, Member States would be liable for any damages caused to individuals by breaches of Community law attributable to the State(4) .

Question No 52 by Karin Riis‐Jørgensen (H−0371/97)

Subject: Future shipbuilding policy

In view of its draft paper of April 1997, how does the Commission see the future of the shipbuilding industry?

When will a stop be put to state aid to shipyards? How does the aid which has just been approved (COM(97)132) square with the recent report on state aid? When does the Commission expect to be able to create equal market conditions for the European shipbuilding industry? When does it anticipate implementing in full the new non‐repeat aid policy, and when does it expect finally to put a stop to exceptional aid packages? How can it ensure that the monitoring of mix‐shipyards is sufficient to prevent public money being transferred, which is already causing problems for the EU?

Answer

Entry into force of the OECD agreement would be the best way of eliminating subsidies and other distortions to competition in the shipbuilding sector. That is why the Commission continues to hope that the US will ratify the agreement. Nevertheless the Community clearly needs to reflect on what approach should be adopted in the event that the OECD agreement does not come into effect. This was the purpose of the Commission working paper to which the Honourable Member refers. At its meeting on 24 April the Industry Council decided that if the OECD agreement has still not yet entered into force the current Community shipbuilding aid directive (which is due to expire at the end of this year) should be extended if necessary until the end of 1998 pending decisions on a specific new regime, on which the Commission will put forward proposals later this year. The extended directive would lapse automatically as soon as the OECD Agreement is ratified by the USA or a new regime on shipbuilding aid policy is adopted. The Commission would expect any such new regime to take into account many of the concerns expressed by the Honourable Member, although the Commission needs to be balanced and realistic at the same time if the overall goal of securing a competitive industry is to be achieved. For example the complex issue of mixed yards needs to be addressed, but it may be difficult to find solutions. The Commission would also seek to enshrine the ‚one time last time’ principle for restructuring aid to shipyards, in line with the approach followed in the specific recent cases covered in document COM (97) 132, to which the Honourable Member also refers, where the governments concerned have undertaken that no more such aids will be granted to the yards involved.

Question No 53 by Fernando Pérez Royo (H−0376/97)

Subject: Agreement on simultaneous fare rises by Spanair, Air Europa and Iberia

The airlines Spanair and Air Europa have expanded their operations throughout Spain in recent years and have dramatically increased their share of the market thanks to their competitive fares. The increase in the number of flights by these and other companies has brought various airports in Spain to saturation point, where they are unable to allow access to other companies, despite the liberalization of the air transport market.

Recently, these two companies, together with Iberia (for its promotional flights), simultaneously increased their fares by 20 %. No action was taken in response to this by the Spanish Government, which should have been aware of the development given that Iberia is a public company. This would suggest that the three companies have reached a price‐fixing agreement, in breach of the rules of free competition and of Article 85(1) of the Treaty.

What measures will the Commission take in response to this alleged agreement?

Question No 54 by Pedro Aparicio Sánchez (H−0377/97)

Subject: Agreement between Iberia, Spanair and Air Europa in breach of Article 85 of the Treaty

The airlines Spanair, Air Europa and Iberia (for its promotional flights) have simultaneously increased their fares by between 15 and 20 %, in what appears to be an agreement to restrict competition in breach of Article 85(1)(a) of the Treaty (directly or indirectly fixing purchase or selling prices) and Article 86 (abuse of a dominant position).

Will the Commission investigate these cases of suspected infringement of the principles of free competition, in accordance with Article 89 of the Treaty?

Joint Answer

With a view to preventing duplicated monitoring procedures and within the framework of the necessary cooperation between national competition authorities and the Commission, the Commission considers it desirable that national competition authorities enforce Articles 85 and 86 of the EC Treaty, potentially in combination with their respective national competition rules, in those cases where certain conditions are fulfilled. In principle, a national competition authority should be responsible for handling those cases where the alleged restrictive practice or agreement bears its effects primarily in the territory of the relevant Member State and where, at first sight, the alleged restrictive practice or agreement does not appear to be entitled to an exemption pursuant to Article 85(3) of the EC Treaty. The Commission reserves, however, the right to intervene in these cases if it judges it necessary.

In the case referred to in the Honourable Member's question, the Spanish competition authority, upon complaint from two regional governments and several consumers' associations, has already initiated a formal investigation procedure with respect to the alleged agreement between Spanair, Air Europa and Iberia to increase their respective tariffs on national flights at the same time.

The characteristics of this case show that the parties to the alleged infringement are established in a sole Member State, Spain; that the effects of the alleged illegal practice are essentially produced within the territory of this Member State, because the tariffs increase basically concerns national routes; and that the alleged infringement, a price fixing, does not appear to be exemptible under Article 85(3)EC.

This case, therefore, should in principle be dealt with by the Spanish competition authorities.

The Commission intends to publish, in the near future, a Communication where it will clearly define the principles and procedures to be applied in the cooperation between the Commission and the competition authorities of the Member States. A draft of this Communication was published on 10.9.1996 (OJ C 262) in order to organize a consultation with the Member States and the parties concerned which has been very fruitful. It is hoped that the Communication itself will be adopted by the Commission and published in the Official Journal before the summer break.

Question No 58 by Patrick Cox (H−0295/97)

Subject: Council Directive 93/104 (working time directive) and its application to the defence forces

Is Council Directive 93/104/EC(5) concerning certain aspects of the organization of working time intended to include members of the defence forces?

Does the Commission consider that Section 3 of the Organization of Working Time Bill (Ireland), which is currently being considered by the Irish Parliament and which precludes members of the defence forces, is compatible with Council Directive 93/104/EC?

Answer

The scope of Directive 93/104/EC is defined by Article 2 of Directive 89/931/EEC on the introduction of measures to encourage improvements in the safety and health of workers.

Article 2(2) of Directive 89/931 states that

’This Directive shall not be applicable where characteristics peculiar to certain specific public service activities, such as the armed forces or the police, or to certain specific activities in the civil protection services inevitably conflict with it.’

Each Member State is clearly in a better position than the Commission to assess, in the light of the arrangements in that Member State, the circumstances and extent to which activities of the armed forces are likely to conflict with the provisions of the Working Time Directive.

Question No 60 by Alfred Lomas (H−0300/97)

Subject: Gibraltar – passports

Will the Commission confirm that the passports issued in Gibraltar to British Dependent Territory Citizens and British Citizens who are EU nationals, and which carry the name Gibraltar on the cover, comply with the Intergovernmental Resolution on the common format of passports, passed in 1981?

Answer

1. As the Honourable Member points out, in 1981 the representatives of the governments of the Member States of the European Communities meeting in the Council adopted a resolution on the common format for passports. That resolution was supported by further resolutions in 1982, 1986 and 1995, to take account in particular of enlargements of the Community.

Those resolutions provide for a model European passport with common features such as the format, colour and some of the information contained therein. Other aspects, however, such as the period of validity of the passport, its security specifications or even its cost, are not covered by the resolutions.

2. Given that the resolutions in question were adopted by the representatives of the governments of Member States meeting in the Council, the legal framework in which they were created does not fall within the European Treaties. For that reason the Commission is not empowered to interpret the resolutions, nor to give a ruling on the conformity of a specific passport with the provisions laid down therein.

However, the Commission points out that, in accordance with the Community law governing the free movement of persons, the Member States are required to issue to their nationals, in accordance with their laws, an identity card or passport specifying, inter alia, their nationality and to renew those cards and passports when necessary. The Member States are also required to admit Union citizens to their territory and to recognize their right to leave it 'on simple presentation of an identity card or a valid passport'.

The Member States must therefore ensure that Union citizens can obtain an identity document within the meaning of Community law in order to exercise their right to freedom of movement.

Question No 61 by Lyndon Harrison (H−0304/97)

Subject: Conservation of tigers

Does the Commission share the widespread concern about the possibility that in a few years tigers will be extinct in the wild? The extent of the problem needs to be identified as well as quantified, in order that a tiger conservation campaign may be established in cooperation with European zoos. Will the Commission seek means to influence the countries where tigers live, in order to impose adequate safeguards against the killing of tigers? If necessary, will it compensate local people who benefit financially from killing these animals? The Commission should encourage European zoos in their efforts to coordinate the preservation of zoo tigers and to develop methods through which tigers may be reintroduced into the wild. Is public interest in the campaign, perhaps through commercial sponsorship by a company with a widely recognized product, going to be promoted? Guidelines for the protection of other endangered, but less prominent, species must be formulated by the Commission as well.

Answer

Yes, The Commission shares the concerns about the threats to the survival of tiger populations in the wild and – together with Member States – has actively participated in discussions and the formulation of recommendations on the issue in the context of the Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of wild fauna and flora (CITES). Tiger conservation will again be a major issue at the 10th meeting of the Conference of the Parties to CITES, to be held in Harare, Zimbabwe, from 9 to 20 June 1997.

The Commission is of the opinion that in situ conservation and enforcement of protective legislation in both range states and consumer countries is essential in order to eradicate poaching and illegal international trade. Breeding of tigers in zoos, however, would appear to be a problem rather than a solution; in fact zoos are taking measures to reduce breeding. The reintroduction of captive bred tigers implies serious genetical risks and would only be useful if in situ protection is guaranteed.

The issue of trade in wild fauna and flora has been dealt with in Community legislation since 1984 (Council Regulation (EEC) No 3626/82; OJ L 384 of 31.1.1982). On 1 June 1997, this legislation will be replaced by Council Regulation (EC) No 338/97; OJ L 61 of 03.03.1997.

Question No 62 by Per Stenmarck (H−0314/97)

Subject: Competition in air transport

The most recent step towards increased competition in civil aviation was taken on 1 April this year.

Is the Commission prepared to go still further in facilitating competition by taking action to bring about a redistribution of slots?

Answer

While the Commission's assessment of aviation liberalisation in Europe is positive with regard to licensing of air carriers, market access and fares, the Commission does recognise that capacity limitations are having an impact at the major airports with capacity problems.

The increasing difficulty in obtaining suitable slots to expand air services is creating barriers to market entry. This restricts competition and protects incumbent carriers. This distortion of the market prevents the full benefits of liberalisation of air transport being achieved. It is therefore necessary to improve the allocation mechanism to facilitate the possibility for slots to change hands in order to improve the efficiency of the air services market and to avoid a static and inflexible situation developing at congested airports which prevents airlines entering the market or increasing frequencies to meet consumer demand.

The Commission is considering different options concerning the necessary revision of the existing slot allocation Regulation. The Commission has studied the situation in detail and consulted all the interested parties in depth. It intends to submit a proposal soon to amend the existing Regulation.

Question No 63 by Per Gahrton (H−0316/97)

Subject: Linda Steneberg's and EU salaries

Following a report in Aftonbladet, Sweden's biggest newspaper, in early April on the tax‐free salaries paid to about 20 Swedes working for the Commission and European Parliament, three Members of the Swedish Parliament, the Green Party spokesman, Birger Schlaug, the former MEP Bengt Olof Ryttar(S) and former Treasury Minister Bo Lundgren (M) voiced their criticisms. This led the head of the Commission office in Stockholm, Linda Steneberg, to write an official letter to the Speaker of the Swedish Parliament, Birgitta Dahl, calling on her to admonish those Members. The Speaker instead replied that it was not only the right but also the duty of Members of the Swedish Parliament to form opinion on various matters of public concern. The case has aroused great interest in Sweden and severe criticism of Linda Steneberg from politicians and the mass media, regardless of their political persuasion and their attitude towards the EU.

Does Linda Steneberg's attempt to muzzle Members of the Swedish Parliament reflect the Commission's? Does Linda Steneberg have the Commission's full confidence?

Answer

Following the publicity in Sweden on the statutes and salaries of Community officials, some Swedish Members of Parliament made statements. A bomb threat was also received in the Commission Office in Stockholm.

Under such serious circumstances, the Head of the Commission's Representation in Sweden considered that it was her duty to react in order to protect the staff and fully clarify this issue. She therefore wrote to the Speaker of the Swedish Parliament on her own initiative to point out that working conditions, salaries and taxes of Community officials are fixed by Community law. This regime had been accepted by Sweden when entering the Community. She also expressed the insult felt by her and the staff when being called a threat against democracy.

When it became clear that the fact of writing this letter led to misinterpretations, the Head of Representation publicly regretted this and felt sorry that her intentions had been misread.

The Commission has full confidence in the way its Head of Representation in Sweden carried out her duties.

Question No 64 by Nikitas Kaklamanis (H−0317/97)

Subject: Turkish 'free zones' and infringement of Customs Union rules

According to constant reports, some of which refer to official documents drawn up by the Council and the Commission's Directorate‐General I, textile products of Asian origin (China, Pakistan etc.) are being imported into Turkish free zones and, although subject to transit regulations, are subsequently exported to the EU as products of Turkish origin.

This is clearly an infringement of the Customs Union agreement between Turkey and the EU, creating a major problem of unfair competition with products from many EU Member States.

What view does the Commission take of this? What are the latest relevant facts at its disposal and what measures will it take if the above information proves to be true?

Answer

The Commission is aware of complaints over the alleged inadequacy of legislation, or implementation of this legislation, on imports of textiles and clothing products from Turkish free zones, which would make it possible to smuggle out of them duty and quota‐free goods of third country origin, which would then be declared of Turkish origin.

However, the Commission is satisfied that legislation on the Turkish free zones is adequate to cover the operations which are at present performed there (essentially customs warehousing and not processing) and is implemented correctly. This is confirmed by the findings of a mission composed of Commission and Member State representatives which took place from 21 to 25 October 1996 and included visits to several free zones as well as meetings with the customs department headquarters in Ankara in order to improve the understanding of the way free zones function with respect to Turkey's commercial policy and customs union obligations. The mission concluded that, from a customs point of view, the situation appears to be satisfactory and that the necessary controls are regularly applied when third country goods are concerned.

The Commission can reassure the Honourable Member of Parliament, that it will continue to deploy all efforts to ensure that the rules governing the EC‐Turkey customs union in the textiles and clothing sector are correctly applied.

Question No 65 by Birgitta Ahlqvist (H−0319/97)

Subject: Development aid to Belarus

Reports last year about the political situation in Belarus were very alarming, with the President assuming increasingly authoritarian powers and the freedom of speech, of the press and of association gradually being eroded. It is now vital to support the development of democratic parties, grassroots movements and free trade unions in Belarus. The President, however, is doing his utmost to stifle democratic debate in the country.

Has the Commission, in the light of the suppression of the political opposition, made adjustments to the development aid granted to Belarus? How can the EU use such aid to counter the rise of an authoritarian state in Belarus?

Answer

The European Union is gravely concerned about the political situation in Belarus, which seriously impairs the prospects for the necessary democratic and economic reform.

For this reason, the European Union has adopted a dual approach towards Belarus. This position adopted by the Council on February 24, 1997 is, on the one hand, no conclusion on the Interim Agreement, no ratification of the Partnership and Co‐operation Agreement, no Joint Committee meeting, no support for Council of Europe membership, no public recognition of the new Constitution and Parliament; but, on the other hand, continue bilateral dialogue and provide assistance with experts in constitutional matters, freedom of media, human rights and economic reform.

In the EU statement on Belarus adopted by the Council on April 28, 1997, the EU is open to establish a framework of dialogue with Belarus in co‐operation with the OSCE and the Council of Europe to regularly review progress in democratic reform.

For the further bilateral dialogue in Minsk; the Commission is ready to contribute to the third Kosto mission, the content of which was proposed in the EU statement on Belarus: i) assisting both the Belarussian authorities and all the deputies of the elected 13th Supreme Soviet in setting up a formal consultation mechanism to discuss proposals for amending the 1994 Constitution, ii) monitoring and facilitating the consultations in co‐ordination with the OSCE and the Council of Europe, iii) reporting to the GAC on the progress made in those consultations.

The Commission would be ready to use Tacis as the main instrument to provide the necessary expertise in the main areas identified by the Council's conclusions.

Question No 66 by John McCartin (H−0320/97)

Subject: Mackerel discards by factory ships

Is the Commission aware of the activities of factory ships fishing for mackerel in the Atlantic waters off Ireland and the UK and has it investigated allegations that in their efforts to obtain uniformed sized mackerel, these ships are discarding as much as three times their landings?

Answer

The main fleets of Community fishing vessels catching mackerel in Western waters off the coasts of the United Kingdom and Ireland are purse seiners and freezer trawlers. In accordance with Article 10(16) of Council Regulation (EEC) No. 3094/86, freezer trawlers may use automatic sorting equipment onboard if its sole function is commercial grading of all the fish caught and intended for freezing. These installations may not be used for automatic discarding of less valuable fish (e.g. small sizes). It is in the first place the responsibility of the Member States to monitor and control their vessels and vessels fishing in their waters and to take the necessary legal steps whenever an infringement of Community legislation occurs.

The Commission has in the past participated in inspections at sea to investigate the activities of pelagic freezer vessels. Following these inspection missions, the Commission has no evidence to confirm the allegations mentioned by the honourable Member concerning the amount of discards. On the vessels in question the sorting machines were installed on the processing deck and did not allow for direct discards. Furthermore, the Commission inspectors did not observe in these cases excessive discards.

Nevertheless, the Commission continues to be attentive to the problem of potential misuse of automatic sorting equipment for discarding onboard such vessels and will carry out additional missions on this subject.

Question No 67 by Ivar Virgin (H−0324/97)

Subject: Transport of animals

The Swedish media has reported that animals are still being transported from the EU to third countries in conditions which in no way meet the requirements for them to be treated decently. Current rules are such that they promote the transport of live animals, regardless of the suffering caused to them.

What steps will the Commission take to guarantee that animals are transported from the EU to third countries in acceptable conditions which meet high animal welfare standards?

Answer

The Commission has become increasingly concerned about the welfare situation of animals traded between the Community and third countries and vice versa following complaints received from animal welfare interests. Although Directive 91/628/EEC contains provisions to protect transported animals both within Community territory an also where part of the journey involves the territory of third countries, it cannot be denied that problems of inadequate enforcement by the responsible authorities and inadequate respect of the rules of commercial operators have occurred.

Particular difficulties are involved in trade with third countries as, of course, Community directives do not directly apply as law within those countries.

Directive 91/628/EEC, as amended by Directive 95/29/EC however, contains provisions designed to ensure the respect by transport operators transporting animals from or to Community territory of adequate welfare conditions for the animals. For example, the authorities of the exporting Member State must ensure that no animal shall be transported unless it is fit for the particular journey involved and suitable provisions have been made for its care during the journey and in case of exports on arrival at the place of destination.

Under these provisions the authorities of a Member State should normally be able to ensure that the operator carrying out the export or import will be obliged to respect the provisions of the directive throughout the journey and carry out the transport in lorries and vessels which are adequate for the purpose. In view of the recent complaints which have been made, the Commission intends to intervene further with the authorities of the Member States concerned (Germany and Italy).

If a Member State consistently fails to adequately enforce the respect of a directive the Commission may of course open the infringements proceedings provided for in Article 169 of the Treaty. The Commission will consider the opening of this procedure in the cases referred to in the present question if on the examination of those cases it appears that the incidents have occurred owing to inadequate enforcement of the directive by the authorities of the Member States involved.

As far as the Commission's controls are concerned, both within and outside the Community, the Commission's product quality control and audit office has been integrated into the Directorate General for Consumer Policy and Health Protection and will be transferred from Brussels to Dublin in the course of this year. Animal welfare will be included in this new structure.

However, the Commission has to point out the limits of the Community competence to impose Community legislation and the practical control of it outside the Community.

In order to increase the degree of protection for animals transported to third countries, the Council received recently a proposal for a Council regulation concerning the connection between granting of export refunds for live animals and the compliance with provisions established in Community legislation.

At a technical level, discussions have already started with experts from Member States to discuss the implementation modalities of such a proposal.

Question No 68 by José Torres Couto (H−0328/97)

Subject: Transport and infrastructure

The Portuguese Government has decided to modify the positions of the previous government with regard to transport accessibility and infrastructure, and in the context of links with the rest of Europe it is now opting for intermodal corridors.

What is the Commission's assessment of this change of position?

Answer

The list of 14 TEN priority projects was endorsed, as the honourable Member will recall, by the European Council in Essen in December 1994 and project No. 8 on that list related to a ‚Lisbon‐Valladolid Motorway’.

The Portuguese Government has since altered its priorities in transport policy and is now seeking to put more emphasis on transport modes such as railways, seaports and airports. In agreement with the Spanish Government, it consequently proposed to the European Council that the priority project be changed.

The European Council at Dublin concluded that ‚It agrees with the proposal by the Portuguese and Spanish Governments that priority project No. 8 in List 1 from the Essen European Council will become the Multimodal link Portugal/Spain with the rest of Europe’.

The Commission considers that the planned change to a ‚multimodal’ project is in line with the objectives of the common transport policy of the Community.

The project has also been examined by the Commission in the light of the specific criteria given in the Decision on the TEN Guidelines. The revised project includes several railway, maritime and air schemes and, can be considered as similar to the Nordic Triangle Project (Project No 12 on the list) which is also, by definition, multimodal. The Commission is of the view that the components of the project as a whole comply with the objectives and criteria set out in the Guidelines. In particular the various elements are included in the maps of Annex I and correspond to the criteria and specifications in Annex II.

As the European Council's decision implies that the description of project No. 8 in Annex III of the Guidelines should be modified, the Commission will therefore include this alteration amongst a number of amendments to the Guidelines which it intends to propose in the course of this year.

Any modification to Annex III and Article 19 of the Decision would of course be made under the co‐decision procedure and in line with the understanding reached in the context of the conciliation procedure on the indicative and political character of Annex III.

Question No 69 by Elly Plooij‐van Gorsel (H−0329/97)

Subject: Health risk to coeliacs through the use of wheat gluten in surface coatings for cheese

Is the Commission aware that the Netherlands dairy industry intends to replace plastic coatings for cheese by a wheat gluten coating which is environmentally friendlier and cheaper?

Is the Commission aware that the various national coeliacs societies, which have joined forces in the Association of European Coeliacs Societies, are very alarmed at this proposal, since there is a risk that gluten from the coating will get into the cheese, for example when it is cut, further reducing the range of foods which can be eaten by coeliacs (people allergic to gluten)?

Is the novel foods regulation, and, in particular, the provisions on the labelling of food products, applicable in this specific case?

If not, what scope does the Commission see for measures to protect the health of coeliacs?

Answer

The Commission was recently informed of the fact that the coating of certain cheeses at present made of plastic might be replaced by a coating made using flour containing gluten.

The Commission is aware of the difficulties which such a change in composition could bring for coeliacs, since the present Community provisions on food labelling do not make it possible to inform consumers about the presence of gluten.

This question was discussed with the Member States during the subsequent meeting of the Standing Committee on Foodstuffs held on 15 April. There is also some suggestion that this problem arises also in respect of other foodstuffs, such as coating apples with starch containing gluten.

Within the framework of the general debate currently under way on the labelling of ingredients which might cause allergies or intolerance, some consideration should be given to the need to provide special labelling to inform consumers of the fact that a particular food has been in contact with products containing gluten.

Question No 70 by Christine Oddy (H−0337/97)

Subject: Working Time directive and employment of lecturers

Is the Commission aware that many part‐time lecturers employed by further education colleges in the UK are having their employment passed onto a separate body, the ELS (Education Lecturing Services), so that they are no longer regarded as employed by the colleges, in order to circumvent their employment rights?

What moves will the Commission take to ensure that lecturers' rights to annual paid leave under the Working Time directive will not be circumvented by this action?

Answer

The Commission shares the Honourable Member's concerns regarding the situation of foreign‐language lecturers in the United Kingdom. However, it would like to point out that, in accordance with the case‐law of the Court of Justice concerning the European rules on the safeguarding of employees' rights in the event of transfers of undertakings, businesses or parts of businesses(6) , the rights and obligations arising out of a contract of employment or an employment relationship may, in the context of a transfer, be changed in respect of the transferee to the same extent as they could have been in respect of the transferor, on the understanding that the transfer of the business may on no account itself constitute the reason for the change. The same principles apply to the right to annual holidays, including the rights laid down in the directive on the organization of working time(7) .

The Commission would like to stress that once the British authorities have transposed Directive 77/187/EEC into national law, it is incumbent on the competent national institutions to check that the national measures implementing that directive are met. In addition, the Commission for its part ensures that the Member States correctly transpose the measures contained in secondary legislation. Only in cases where evidence emerged of delayed transposition, as with the directive on the organization of working time in the United Kingdom, or the incorrect application of the national transposition measures, might the Commission decide, if appropriate, to initiate infringement proceedings pursuant to Article 169 of the EEC Treaty.

Question No 71 by James Nicholson (H−0342/97)

Subject: Special Support Programme for Peace and Reconciliation in Northern Ireland and the border countries of Ireland 1995‐1999

What action is proposed to counter the reduction in available funds to District Partnerships occasioned by the prevailing exchange rate between the ECU and £ Sterling and can additional funds be made available to restore the original value of the fund to those partnerships?

Answer

The amount of assistance to be provided by the Structural Funds to an Operational Programme is fixed in ECUs in the Commission Decision which approves the Programme. There is no provision for adjustment of the amount when exchange rate movements result either in increases or in reductions in the amount of assistance when it is converted into the national currency concerned. All Structural Funds programmes in all Member States are treated equally in this respect.

It is not therefore proposed to increase the Structural Funds' allocation to the Special Support Programme for Peace and Reconciliation in Northern Ireland and the Border Counties of Ireland (1994‐1999), under which District Partnerships are funded, in order to compensate for exchange rate movements.

Question No 72 by Edgar Schiedermeier (H−0343/97)

Subject: European Cohesion Forum

What conclusion were drawn at the European Cohesion Forum organized by the Commission and held in Brussels from 28 to 30 April 1997? Did the Commission present a proposal for the forthcoming reform of the Structural Funds at this conference?

Answer

The Cohesion Forum was organised by the Commission as an opportunity for all concerned by Community cohesion policies – national, regional and local authorities, social partners, representative organisations, research institutes and experts – to give their assessment of achievements up to the present and contribute ideas for the future. While, in view of its nature, there were no formal conclusions or proposals arising from the Forum, the member of the Commission in charge of regional policies in the closing session, outlined the main messages which had emerged. The Commissioner said that the Forum had recognised the key role of cohesion policy as a basic component of the European model of society. Much progress had been achieved in the policies introduced since 1989, but more needed to be done to help the weakest regions, as well as other regions undergoing restructuring, and to improve the quality of Europe's human resources and reduce social exclusion. There was room for improvement in Community cohesion policies, more concentration of resources, with transitional help for areas no longer eligible, improved integration of town and country in order to promote the balanced development of rural regions and a greater focus on the particular problem of urban areas.

The member of the Commission also pointed to the many possibilities for the simplification of the delivery system for Community cohesion policies in order to improve their effectiveness: fewer objectives and fewer Community Initiatives, fewer programmes, quicker procedures, more incentives to promote the efficient use of Community resources, tighter financial controls, wider participation in the partnerships.

The Commission will present its formal proposal for the future financing of Community policies soon after the conclusion of the Inter‐Governmental Conference which is expected to take place in June 1997. This will include guidelines for the future of structural policies taking into account the views expressed at the Cohesion Forum. Following agreement on future financing, the Commission will propose new regulations for the Structural Funds probably in the first half of 1998.

Question No 73 by Ulla Sandbæk (H−0344/97)

Subject: EU support to Community‐based natural resource management in Africa

Meeting in Brussels on 17‐20 March 1997, the ACP‐EU Joint Parliamentary Assembly passed two important resolutions on Southern Africa supporting the region's approach to biodiversity conservation and the rural economic development of its poorest areas. One of the resolutions acknowledges the success that community‐based natural management programmes are having in the region and the ability of such community conservation processes to provide real and tangible benefits to communities through the sustainable use of wild species, while the other requests the European Union to explicitly support African community‐based natural resource management programmes aiming at conserving natural resources through rural communities' empowerment and sustainable use.

As requested by the ACP‐EU Assembly, will the European Commission effectively support community‐based natural resource management programmes such as Zimbabwe's CAMPFIRE, both financially and institutionally?

Would the Commission support such integrated programmes, which are fully in line with the aims of the Convention on Biological Diversity in other areas of Africa where it seems that, so far, no attempt to develop them has been made?

Answer

The European Commission fully supports the general principles of Community‐based wildlife management in Africa. Therefore, both resolutions passed during March 17‐29 1997 by the ACP‐EU Joint Parliamentary Assembly are coherent with the general orientations. Moreover, the recently adopted Council Regulation on ‚Environmental Measures in developing countries in the context of sustainable development’ and the Council Regulation on ‚Actions to promote tropical forests in developing countries’ both indicate the preservation of biological diversity as an area for intervention, and further underline that particular attention will be given to projects from local populations.

The European Commission is currently funding an integrated programme on Community‐based wildlife management in Kenya, Uganda and Tanzania. There is also an ongoing field research in Africa on the socioeconomic impacts of Community‐based wildlife management. A recent EU MS' Experts' Meeting on protected areas will consider further initiatives in this area.

The European Commission could consider supporting Community‐based wildlife management programmes by any eligible organisation as per above‐mentioned Council Regulations.

Question No 74 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0350/97)

Subject: Protection of the archaeological site at Vouthroto in Albania

The distressing events following the crisis in Albania have resulted in the looting of the archaeological site at Vouthroto, which UNESCO designated in 1992 as part of the world cultural heritage. Many of the priceless objects found, which witnesses say are now in the hands of persons unknown, date from the early Bronze Age and include typical pieces from every period of European history – a wealth of sculptures, vases, statuettes, engravings etc. The archive material unearthed by the Greek archaeological expedition carried out under the inter‐governmental protocol on scientific and technological cooperation between Greece and Albania is also exposed to similar risks.

In view of this situation, will the Commission say: what representations it can make to the Albanian authorities to save what they can of the archaeological discoveries, how it might intercede to prevent stolen and looted archaeological treasures from being sold, and how it might assist further excavation when the situation returns to normal?

Answer

The Commission has heard about the looting of the archaeological site at Vouthroto, which UNESCO designated as part of the world cultural heritage. This has happened during the current crisis in Albania. It is important to bear in mind that the region in which the site is situated is still outside the control of the central government. The multinational defence force in Albania is rather concentrated on the urban areas and the main lines of communication and can scarcely therefore protect the site. Nor has it any mandate to act as a police force (only to provide assistance for humanitarian aid and defence of the international consultative mission). There is therefore little possibility of taking action in the immediate future. However, as part of its contacts with the new reconciliation government, the Commission intends to question the government on this matter and on the measures to be taken.

One other possible course of action would be to swiftly reinstate the customs services and thus set up checks on any cross‐border movements of the goods in question in this specific case. Since customs duties represent an important resource for Albania, action taken in this field – apart from humanitarian aid already given – are in any case one of the priority activities. Following an initial general evaluation mission in Albania last week with a view to getting the PHARE programme restarted, the Commission will draw up a plan of action for the customs in cooperation with the Albanian authorities.

Moreover, as soon as conditions allow, the Commission is intending also to re‐launch activities within the specific ‚cross‐border’ (Greece/Albania/Italy) PHARE programme. In this regard the Commission will examine the possibility of keeping a group of experts at the Vouthroto site to assess the damage suffered and to set up emergency protection measures. This group should operate and these emergency measures should be carried out in close cooperation with the Albanian authorities and under the auspices of an international organization which is competent in the matter such as ICOMOS. When the situation returns to normal in the area, the ‚cross‐border’ PHARE programme could also include promotion and continuation of archaeological digs at the Vouthroto site.

Question No 75 by Eva Kjer Hansen (H−0351/97)

Subject: Danish legislation on second homes and the protocol on the acquisition of second homes in the Maastricht Treaty

The Danish Association of persons letting summer cottages has for a number of years been seeking to have the authorized letting period extended and spread out. Current Danish rules on letting are being interpreted by the Danish Ministry of the Environment as no more than 26 plus 4 weeks. The Ministry's argument has been that extending and spreading the letting period would create doubt over the Danish protocol in the Treaty and that lengthening the period would make the letting out of second homes a commercial activity. To clarify the matter, in March 1997 the Commission replied to questions from the Association.

Can the Commission confirm that the matter of any lengthening of letting periods for second homes is an internal Danish one, which does not include the acquisition of second homes, and that a change of this kind would have no effect on the Danish protocol in the Treaty on the acquisition of second homes in Denmark?

Will the Commission give its views on whether any extension of the letting period would mean that second homes acquired commercial status, and whether this would have any effect on the Danish protocol in the Treaty?

Answer

The purchase of real estate in another Member State is governed by Treaty provisions on the free movement of capital. Apart from transitional periods for new Member States and the provisions in specific protocols on the Åland Islands and Denmark, this right is guaranteed throughout the Community. As with the other freedoms, it is conditioned by the right of Member States to take measures which are justified on grounds of public policy or public security. Other Treaty rights which should be borne in mind in any consideration of this topic are the free movement of persons and the freedom of establishment and to provide services, since these can also have implications for the acquisition of property.

In the Danish case, the right for other EU citizens to acquire property, generally a capital movement, but sometimes a domestic operation (e.g. an EU citizen working or doing business from Denmark) is conditioned by the Protocol to the Treaty on the acquisition of property in Denmark which states that ‚notwithstanding the provisions of this Treaty, Denmark may maintain the existing legislation on the acquisition of second homes’. This was the formulation requested and obtained by the Danish authorities in the revision to the Treaty. The Commission has never contested the right of the Danish authorities to apply this legislation.

A separate issue is the use of second homes. In Denmark, as in other Member States, there are specific national rules governing the rights and duties of real estate owners. The Commission, in accordance with the principle of subsidiarity, regards such matters as primarily within the domain of national regulation where such rules bear equally on nationals and other EU citizens.

The Commission met and corresponded with representatives of Danish summer house owners recently and expressed the view that the case of the maximum winter letting period for second homes was a matter for internal Danish legislation. This legislation governs the use, rather than the conditions of acquisition, of these homes and, as such, would not, in the Commission's view, represent a change in the ‚existing legislation on acquisition’ referred to in the protocol. On the question of an extension to the winter letting possibilities implying that this then became a commercial activity, they are not entirely clear why letting for 52 weeks should be any different from letting for 30 weeks per year, and how such an extension would automatically give a right to ownership. However, the Danish authorities would appear to hold a different view. This view may be based on the possibilities given in ‚existing legislation’ for operation in real estate linked to Treaty freedoms, other than those on capital movements. It should be noted that neither the Commission or the Danish authorities can claim any authentic interpretation of Treaty provisions, since this role is entrusted to the European Court of Justice were it to deliberate on the issues in question.

On a broader economic level it should, perhaps, be noted that while the possibilities given by the Treaty to restrict acquisition may depress the price of such homes to the benefit of Danish residents seeking to acquire them, the counterpart is a corresponding lower asset value for those owning such homes, as well as a lower potential return on the asset due to the short letting period allowed.

***

Question No 76 by Konstantinos Hatzidakis (H−0354/97)

Subject: Low take‐up rate for the 'Kleisthenis' programme in the Greek Community Support Framework

The 'Kleisthenis' programme forms an important part of the Second Community Support Programme for Greece: it is intended to modernize Greek public administration which constitutes one of the main priorities in Greek attempts to promote development and convergence with the other Community countries. However, various sources indicate that for over three years there have been serious delays in the take‐up of the relevant Community funds, so that the programme has been implemented very slowly. Will the Commission say precisely how long these delays have been, and what measures it intends to take to ensure that the programme can be implemented more rapidly?

Answer

The absorption situation of the Operational Programme for the ‚Modernisation of Public Administration’ (best known as ‚Klisthenis’) is as follows: sub‐programme 1: 86 % of 1994‐96 total cost – 37 % of 1994‐99 total cost; subprogramme 2: 31 % of 1994‐96 total cost – 6 % of 1994‐99 total cost; sub‐programme 3: 47 % of 1994‐96 total cost – 12 % of 1994‐99 total cost; sub‐programme 4: 51 % of 1994‐96 total cost – 9 % of 1994‐99 total cost.

Thus sub‐programme 1, which is related purely to training, presents a more or less normal rate of absorption. Subprogramme 3, related purely to infrastructure (mainly acquiring and installation of equipment), presents a lower rate. This is explained by the major and lengthy organisational and administrative changes (requiring in certain cases complete legislative procedures) required before beginning actual implementation of the relevant projects. Normally, absorption performance of this sub‐programme will have decisively improved by the first half of 1998.

Sub‐programme 2 presents an even lower rate of absorption. However, low absorption performance of measures 2.1 and 2.3 of this sub‐programme, concerning infrastructure, is justified by the same reasons as for subprogramme 3. On the other hand, measures 2.2 concerning modernisation studies and 2.4 concerning the national initiative ‚Kapodistrias’, launched only recently by the Greek Government, need closer attention and concentration of efforts in order to absorb funds in an efficient and productive way. The same remark applies to sub‐programme 4 which concerns technical assistance.

The Commission expects to be informed on progress here at the next meeting of the Monitoring Committee later this month. It is also possible that by the end of 1997 the Monitoring Committee of the programme will decide on any corrective adjustments necessary for obtaining the best results from ‚Klisthenis’.
