Subject: Use of standards for the transmission of television channels

In view of the Spanish Parliament's completion of the procedure concerning the law on the use of standards for the transmission of television signals, does the Commission consider that its content complies with Directive 95/47(8) on the use of standards for the transmission of television signals, specifically as regards the simulcrypt and multicrypt processes and the standardization and certification of equipment?

When will the Commission give its opinion on this matter?

Question No 78 by Joan Colom i Naval (H−0357/97)

Subject: Legislation on television decoders

In its reply to questions H−0049/97 and H−0079/97(9) the Commission summarized the main features of European legislation governing the transmission of television signals and in particular digital television services.

On 17 April 1997 the Spanish Congress of Deputies completed the passage into law of Decree‐Law 1/97, which was the subject of my previous question.

Can the Commission now say whether the text of that Spanish law, including the transitional arrangements, complies with Directive 95/47/EC(10) and with Community legislation on free competition?

Joint answer

Members will recall that Directive 95/47/EC requires the use of standardised transmission systems for digital television, and the incorporation of a descrambler conforming to the Common European Scrambling System in all relevant consumer equipment.

The Directive also requires that for pay‐television services, conditional access providers offer their services to broadcasters on fair, reasonable and non‐discriminatory terms.

The monitoring of transposition into national law of Community directives is a formal process involving technical and legal studies carried out by the Commission of the relevant legislation. The process has already started in connection with the Television Standards Directive in the case of a number of Member States, including Spain. Upon completion of these studies, the Commission will make known its position on the Spanish legislation.

Question No 80 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−0361/97)

Subject: Dangers arising from the cultivation and production of genetically modified tomatoes

The authorization given to the multinational company 'Zeneca' to go ahead with the experimental cultivation of genetically modified tomatoes in three prefectures in Greece has triggered a storm of protest in Greek public opinion which is very alarmed at this prospect. Modern cultivation practices have led to the disappearance of many natural varieties of tomato, and total production of fresh tomatoes and a large proportion of production of tomatoes destined for processing come from hybrids rather than common varieties; this means that there is less potential for growing local varieties which are adapted to the climatological and soil conditions of each region, and the reduction in varieties makes the total disappearance of natural tomatoes a very real possibility.

Does the Commission intend to take immediate practical measures – such as the activation of Directive 90/220(11) – in order strictly to ban the export, production and marketing of genetically modified organisms in Europe, given that – as the example of genetically modified maize has shown – there is a very serious danger that the modified product will develop allergies, create resistant strains of bacteria, exterminate traditional varieties, distort the composition of natural fauna, turn whole areas into experimental laboratories, affect the health and life of consumers to an incalculable extent, concentrate production in the hands of a few people and lead to job losses and the disappearance of traditional natural crops and entire agricultural regions and populations?

Answer

The experimental release of genetically modified tomatoes notified to the Greek Competent Authorities has been performed in accordance with the requirements of part B of Directive 90/220/EEC on the deliberate release into the environment of genetically modified organisms. There have been 58 notifications concerning experimental releases of genetically modified tomatoes in the Community in 6 other Member States (Spain, Italy, France, Holland, Portugal and the UK) in the period 1991 to 1997. The majority of the notifications concerns tomatoes modified for altered ripening, altered processing characteristics and increased insect and disease resistance. No adverse human or environmental effects have been observed from this work.

Part C of Directive 90/220/EEC which has been used for the placing on the market of products containing or consisting of genetically modified organisms such as the Ciba Geigy (now Novartis) maize, does not contain a mechanism for the banning of such products in the absence of safety grounds affecting environmental or human safety. There is no evidence that the genetically modified maize from Ciba Geigy has been associated with any adverse environmental or human safety issues and accordingly still has a consent to be placed on the market under Directive 90/220/EEC.

The Commission has not, to date (7 May 1997), received any notification under part C of Directive 90/220/EEC for the placing on the market of genetically modified tomatoes and accordingly no authorization under the Directive has been made. For such an authorization, the applicant would have to provide a risk assessment of any safety issues for environmental and human health under Directive 90/220/EEC or relevant sectorial legislation such as the novel food regulation. Accordingly at this time these tomatoes cannot be marketed in the Community.

Question No 81 by Ulf Holm (H−0366/97)

Subject: Lobbyists in the Commission

There has been a fairly wide‐ranging debate over the past few years concerning lobbyists in EU institutions. The European Parliament is now compiling a register in which all lobbyists are to be listed. The relevant parliamentary committee is soon to submit further proposals for rules governing matters such as gifts, meals, etc. in order to regulate lobbying.

Does the Commission keep a similar register of lobbyists seeking to influence Commissioners or Commission officials?

Does the Commission have rules on lobbyists?

Answer

The Commission has no register equivalent to that envisaged by the European Parliament. In fact the Commission advocates as open a dialogue as possible with lobbyists and therefore does not impose any system of registration.

On the other hand the Commission has just published its first list containing over 600 pan‐European non‐profit making organizations with which it is associated. The purpose behind publication of this list is to make the institutions' officials more aware of the importance of consulting lobbyists by bringing lesser known and therefore less frequently consulted organizations to greater prominence and attention. The list is a working document, not a seal of approval; it is purely for information. It tells the reader ‚who is who’ and ‚who does what’ within the nonprofit making organizations. Listing does not imply any official recognition by the Commission, nor does it bring any privileges. Interested organizations place themselves upon the list on a voluntary basis following publication of an announcement in the Official Journal(12) and on the basis of the Commission's fragmented data.

The Commission has no set rules as regards lobbyists (approval, registration, code of conduct). The approach taken by the Commission set out in its Communication of December 1992(13) entitled ‚Open and structured dialogue between the Commission and lobbyists’ encourages the lobbyists to be self‐regulating on the basis of a certain number of minimum criteria published in the communication. It did not therefore wish to impose any code of conduct on the lobbyists, regarding them as being in the best position to do that.

Question No 82 by Ioannis Theonas (H−0368/97)

Subject: Consequences for Greece arising from the recent publication of the general economic outlook for 1997

In its recent report on the general economic outlook for 1997, the Commission states that only Greece and Italy will be unable to meet the 3 % public deficit target. In the light of this forecast, the Commission calls on Greece to redouble its efforts to achieve the 3 % target, notably by intensifying its austerity programme and expanding the tax basis, further reducing public spending and extending privatization.

Does the Commission not agree that such a policy will finally sacrifice the objective of development and increase disparities between countries such as Greece and the remaining Member States, thereby putting paid to any idea of convergence? Does it not agree also that this policy will turn unemployment into an explosive issue and plunge other population groups into poverty and marginalization, resulting in a further decline in consumption and investment and, consequently, the loss of even more jobs?

Answer

On 23 April 1997, the ‚Spring 1997 Economic Forecasts for the period 1996‐1998’ prepared by the Commission were released. According to these forecasts, the general government deficit in Italy and Greece is expected to be higher than 3 % of GDP in 1997; in the case of Greece, the deficit should reach 4.9 % of GDP. This figure compares with the objective of 4.2 % of GDP adopted by the Greek authorities in the convergence programme and in the budget for 1997: clearly, both the Commission and the Greek authorities forecast a general government deficit for this year higher than 3 % of GDP. However, there should be no confusion between the present forecasts and the decisions concerning which Member States will participate in the monetary union on 1 January 1999; these decisions will be taken in Spring 1998 on the basis of the results for 1997 available at that time.

In the ‚Broad Economic Policy Guidelines’, drawn up in line with Article 103(2) of the Treaty, to be approved by the European Council at Amsterdam (in June 1997), the Commission, on the basis of the Spring Economic Forecasts, states that in Greece ‚sustained efforts on a wide range of fronts are required in order to meet the targets of the convergence programme, ...’; it is recalled that the targets of the convergence programme for the deficit are 4.2 % of GDP in 1997 and 2.4 % of GDP in 1998.

As regards the impact on growth and economic development of a policy of continuing budgetary consolidation for Member States like Greece, the Commission notes that Greece made considerable progress in recent years in correcting fiscal imbalances while at the same time recovery in economic activity was compatible with decelerating inflation. Furthermore, the rate of growth in employment allowed for the almost stabilization of the unemployment rate. In the last two years, growth in Greece was driven by the recovery in domestic demand, both private consumption and investment; consumption was supported by the increase in real disposable income, despite rising direct taxes; recovery in investment, both in construction and equipment, reflected the positive impact of sustained decline in interest rates and of inflows of Structural Funds. In the medium term, the pursuit of sounder budgetary positions reducing inflation expectations and interest rates, will allow for higher rates of non‐inflationary growth. This policy proves to be a sustainable solution to expand employment and to improve social conditions in Greece.

Question No 83 by Inger Schörling (H−0373/97)

Subject: Use of TRI as a solvent

There are good health‐related and environmental reasons why TRI has been banned as a solvent in Sweden. Moreover, adequate alternatives that cause less damage to health and the environment are available for uses of TRI.

Why has the Commission not prohibited the use of TRI as a solvent throughout the EU? Does it not understand that the EU will be seen as obstructing efforts to improve the environment and look after human health if Sweden is forced to lift its ban on the use of TRI owing to rules on the free movement of goods?

Answer

The Commission refers the honourable Member to its answer to Oral Question H−0759/96 by Mr Gahrton.

The Swedish measures to prohibit the sale, transfer and use of methylene chloride and trichloroethylene for industrial purposes fall within the scope of Article 30 of the EC Treaty which prohibits all measures having equivalent effect to quantitative restrictions on imports. A Member State may in certain cases rely on Article 36 of the EC Treaty or mandatory requirements recognized by the Court of Justice, inter alia public health or the environment, to justify derogations from the principle of free movement of goods. The Court of Justice has consistently held that it is for the national authorities that invoke Article 36 or any mandatory requirement to prove in each specific case that a measure that hinders intra‐Community trade is necessary for the effective protection of such a requirement and that the import of the product concerned would constitute a serious risk. Under the principle of proportionality, the national measures have to be proportionate to the objective to be attained, i.e. to public health or the environment.

The Commission sent a letter of formal notice to Sweden on 9 October 1996 concerning the Swedish measures on methylene chloride and trichloroethylene. In its reply, received by the Commission on 6 January 1997, Sweden rejected the Commission's arguments and restated its position that the Swedish measures are needed to protect the health of workers.

Trichloroethylene, a solvent mainly used for metal degreasing by professional users, is classified as carcinogen category 3. Category 3, which is the lowest category, means that there is a cause for concern for man owing to possible carcinogenic effects but in respect of which the available information is not adequate for making a satisfactory assessment.

A risk assessment is being carried out under Regulation 793/93 on existing substances. The risk assessment report will describe the risk for the environment, the public and workers from exposure to trichloroethylene. In the draft presented by the UK it is indicated that certain occupational exposure situations may pose risks in poorly controlled workplaces. However, the conclusion cannot yet be drawn that industrial uses of trichloroethylene should be restricted at Community level.

In short, the Commission does see a certain risk and considers that the exposure of workers to trichloroethylene, as for many other chemicals used by industry, needs to be controlled. There is however a wide range of options available to control exposures in the workplace.

Trichloroethylene is on the Commission's priority list for the development of occupational exposure limits (OEL) for chemical agents at work. In the course of this work all currently available data concerning risks to workers' health will be evaluated. The Commission may subsequently propose appropriate legislative measures within the framework of the existing legislation on occupational safety and health.

In its letter of formal notice, the Commission considered that a ban on the placing on the market of trichloroethylene is excessive in relation to the risk.

Question No 85 by Anne McIntosh (H−0002/97)

Subject: Changes in European Union law regarding sex discrimination

In view of the recent amendment to the 1986 Equal Treatment Directive, concerning levels of occupational pensions, will the European Commission take this opportunity to fully explain the rationale behind the changes?

Can the Commission indicate the extent to which the equalization of occupational pensions will affect the average pension, and whether this will go up or down?

If the trend is to push the average pension up, what assurances can the European Commission give that it will not result in job losses as businesses seek to offset these higher costs?

Answer

The amendment to which the honourable Member refers does not concern levels of occupational pensions. It simply seeks to bring Directive 86/378 into line with Article 119 of the EC Treaty, as interpreted by the Court of Justice in the Barber and subsequent judgments.

In this case law, the Court interpreted Article 119 of the Treaty in such a way that discrimination between men and women in occupational social security schemes was prohibited in general. The case law automatically invalidated certain provisions of Council Directive 86/37/EEC of 24 July 1986.

The case law and the amended directive require only that there be no discrimination. They do not in principle require ‚levelling up’ to the level of the hitherto more favoured group, nor do they require the setting of particular levels of pension.

Question No 86 by Manuel Medina Ortega (H−0217/97)

Subject: Fraud against the Community transit system in the banana sector

In its report submitted to Parliament on 12 March 1997 the Committee of Inquiry into the Community transit system states that fraud in the banana sector totals ECU 15 m (A4‐0053/97, Volume I and Volume IV). Could the Commission explain how this fraud has been carried out and what measures it has taken or plans to take to curb it?

Answer

Fraudulent abuse of the transit system in the banana sector was first discovered by the Commission in collaboration with the Italian authorities in 1992. Substantial quantities of South American bananas were shipped to northern Community ports and declared for export to third countries. A T.1 transit procedure was opened with the Customs Office of Milan declared as office of destination. The bananas were in reality directly delivered to two Italian end‐buyers and described as being of Community origin in order to be sold on the internal market at a low cost. The transit documents for the banana consignments were falsely discharged at the Customs Office of Milan. Counterfeit return copies of T.1 documents were forwarded to customs offices of departure to release the guarantees imposed on the transit operations.

Another fraud was detected in 1993. The same individuals involved in the previous case organized new consignments from northern Community ports to third countries such as Albania and Turkey via Austria, Germany and Italy. The goods were irregularly withdrawn from the EC transit regime in Italy. To avoid seizure of guarantees at the customs offices of departure, the organization established two transit procedures; a first transit from Belgium to Germany or from Germany to Austria and another transit from Germany and Austria to Italian ports where the transit documents were falsely discharged. The bananas went directly to the Italian end‐buyers. The fraud mechanism was discovered when an unusual number of bananas in transit via Germany was identified by the Italian customs.

As a result of these investigations, all the main participants in the criminal networks responsible for dispatching bananas into the Community have been arrested and their assets have been seized to ensure payment of customs duties totalling 15 MECU.

The Commission described briefly these fraud cases in its 1995 annual report on the fight against fraud.

The Commission would emphasize that, in line with its communications since March 1995, considerable attention has been paid to bring about improvements in the transit procedures until the adoption on 30 April of this year of an Action Plan for making these procedures both more efficient and more fraud‐proof. It is particularly important to bear in mind that bananas are considered within the framework of emergency measures of protection to be sensitive products for which the use of the comprehensive guarantee is forbidden.

***

Question No 87 by Marialiese Flemming (H−0260/97)

Subject: Nuclear power stations

When is it planned to close the nuclear power stations currently in operation in Europe, what programmes already exist for this, what cost estimates are available, and what measures are planned for the safe disposal of the waste occurring?

Answer

At the moment there are eight Member States which use nuclear power stations for the production of electricity – Germany, Belgium, Spain, Finland, France, the Netherlands, the United Kingdom and Sweden – with a net total capacity of approximately 126 Gwe. Significant reductions are envisaged for Sweden, following a decision by the national Parliament to cease the nuclear production of electricity by the year 2010, for the Netherlands, whose sole nuclear power station is to be shut down between 2000 and 2005 and for the United Kingdom, which is to cease using Magnox and AGR reactors by 2020.

In the absence of any orders for new nuclear power stations in future decades, the great majority of nuclear power stations in service, the largest of which began production between 1977 and 1997 (and a power station with a capacity of 1455 MWe is to be started up in France in 1998) will reach the end of their lives between 2015 and 2040.

Most Member States follow the policy of making nuclear power stations safe for a period of approximately 40 years before dismantling them, in order to ensure that the radionuclides with a short half‐life have decayed.

Taking account of the nuclear power stations already shut down, it may be envisaged that the production of radioactive waste between 2020 and 2100 will be 3 000 m3‐15 000 m3 per plant, depending on the type of plant and other factors.

Dismantling costs are of the order of 15 % of building costs and are revised as a function of inflation rates. Financing comes from reserves accumulated during the useful life of the power stations in the form of funds invested under government control or operators' internal reserves.

Disposal of radioactive waste produced may be carried out within the plants such as those already in service in most countries concerned – Centre de l'Aube in France, El Cabril in Spain, VLJ and Losi in Finland, Eram in Germany, Drigg and Dounreay in the United Kingdom and SFR in Sweden.
